His statement: (and it is that which is used on the body, etc.) Its apparent meaning is that if he washes a limb of his body for other than janabah or impurity, it becomes used. The most correct is that it does not become used due to lack of fulfilling the obligation, as in al-Bahr. His statement: (for removing an event, even if he does not intend by it a pious act, like the ablution of a hadath without intention, by consensus on the correct view). The author did not mention what is used to fulfill an obligation, such as washing some limbs of his ablution, for it becomes used due to fulfillment of the obligation by agreement, even if it does not remove the event from his limb because it is known that removal and establishment are not divisible, and there is no necessary connection between fulfillment of obligation and removal of the event because the import of fulfillment is non-obligation of repetition, and removal of the event depends on completion. His statement: (the pious act) is an action for which one is rewarded, and there is no reward except with intention. His statement: (seeking closeness to become an act of worship). But if he performed ablution in another gathering and did not intend closeness, it is wasteful and the water is not considered used. His statement: (if it is in one gathering) means and he did not perform with the first an act of worship for which purification is prescribed, otherwise it is not disliked. His statement: (disliked) means even if he intended closeness, and it is wasteful, and waste is haram, even on the bank of a river, as said by al-Sayyid, and its purport is that the dislike is tahrimi. The most correct is that the wash water of the deceased if there is no impurity on him is used, like the ablution of a menstruating woman with the intention of performing a mustahabb act, for the water becomes used thereby. His statement: (like washing a garment and an edible animal) meaning both are pure. They said that the sweat of a donkey is pure, and if a dog shakes off water and it hits a person, it does not make him impure because it is pure in itself. The implication of this is that if he washes them, their wash water is pure and is purifying due to absence of cause for being used. His statement: (on the correct view) is what the general public holds, and it is confirmed in al-Hidayah and many books that it is the madhhab, as in al-Bahr, and its reasoning is what the author mentioned by his statement 'and its fall, etc.' Al-Tahawi and some scholars of Balkh chose that it does not become used except when it settles. The benefit of the difference appears when it separates and does not settle, then falls on another limb and flows over it without him taking it with his hand. According to the first, washing that limb with that water is not valid; according to the second, it is valid. Know that concerning the description of used water, some have reported a difference on three narrations. The scholars of Iraq said: no difference is established at all; rather it is pure, not purifying according to all our companions. Shaykh al-Islam said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: it is the chosen view with us, and it is what is mentioned in most of Muhammad's books from our companions, and the verifiers among the scholars of Transoxiana chose it. It is said in al-Mujtaba: the narrations are confirmed from all that it is pure, not purifying, except al-Hasan, and his narration is anomalous and not acted upon, as in Majma' al-Anhur. However, drinking it and kneading with it is disliked tanzihan due to the soul's aversion. His statement: (meaning it is not valid) he explained it thus because, if he left it...

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the ruling on musta'mal (used) water. The matn defined it as water used on the body to remove hadath (ritual impurity). The commentary explains that if water is used for a non-obligatory wash (e.g., washing for coolness), it does not become musta'mal according to the most correct view. Water becomes musta'mal only when it fulfills an obligation (like ablution) or is used for a pious act (qurbah). For ablution without intention, it becomes used because it removes hadath. If one washes only some limbs, it becomes used due to fulfilling the obligation, even though hadath is not fully removed. The commentary also discusses the ruling when the same ablution is done in one gathering vs. separate gatherings—doing it twice in one gathering is wasteful and thus makruh tahrimi. Washing hands for food with sunnah intention makes the water used; otherwise not. The correct view is that water becomes used upon separation from the body, not upon settling (as al-Tahawi held). The difference matters if separated water falls on another limb without being taken by hand. The established Hanafi position is that musta'mal water is pure (tahir) but not purifying (ghayr mutahhir), and this is the relied-upon view. Drinking it is disliked tanzihan due to aversion.

Key Terms
مستعمل (musta'mal): Water that has been used for ritual purification (ablution or bathing) and has separated from the body.
طاهر غير مطهر (tahir ghayr mutahhir): Pure in itself but not capable of removing ritual impurity.

Key Takeaway
Water becomes musta'mal only when used for a ritual purification that fulfills an obligation or a pious act, and it separates from the body.

The Ruling of Musta'mal Water and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on the ruling and dispute regarding musta'mal water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi ruling that musta'mal water is tahir ghayr mutahhir and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the moment it becomes used.

Core Idea
Musta'mal water is pure but not purifying; the dispute between separation vs. settling has practical consequences.

Having established when water becomes musta'mal, we now examine its legal classification: is it purifying or not? The Hanafi school holds it is pure but not purifying, a point of consensus among the majority. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the exact moment it becomes used—upon separation or upon settling—which affects practical cases.

Guided Path
1
State the established ruling
Musta'mal water is tahir (pure) but ghayr mutahhir (not purifying). This is the madhhab.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Tahawi and Balkh scholars hold that water becomes used only upon settling; the majority say upon separation.
The dispute is about the moment, not the final status.
3
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
If water separates and falls on another limb without being taken by hand, according to the majority, that water is musta'mal and cannot be used for purification; according to al-Tahawi, it is not yet used and can be used.
This practical difference matters in daily wudu.
4
Evaluate the evidence
The majority view is based on the principle that water becomes used when the obligation is fulfilled. Al-Tahawi's view requires stabilization.
The reasoning is talil (rationale).
5
Note the authority weight
The majority view is relied upon (mufta bihi), confirmed by al-Hidayah, Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, and the scholars of Iraq and Transoxiana. Al-Hasan's anomalous narration is not adopted.
This gives tarjih to the separation view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وهو ما استعمل في الجسد الخ) ظاهره إنه إذا غسل عضواً من جسده لغير جنابة، ونجاسة يكون مستعملاً، والأصح أنه لا يكون مستعملاً لعدم إسقاط الفرض كما في البحر قوله: (لرفع حدث وإن لم ينو بذلك قربة، كوضوء المحدث بلا نية إجماعاً على الصحيح، ولم يذكر المصنف ما استعمل لإسقاط فرض بأن غسل بعض أعضاء وضوئه، فإن يصير مستعملاً لسقوط الفرض اتفاقاً، وإن لم يزل به حدث عضوه لما عرف أنه لا يتجزأ زوالاً وثبوتاً، ولا تلازم بين سقوط الفرض، وزوال الحدث لأن مفاد السقوط عدم وجوب الإعادة، ورفع الحدث موقوف على التمام قوله : (القربة) هي فعل ما يثاب عليه، ولا ثواب إلا بالنية قوله : (تقرباً ليصير عبادة أما إذا توضأ في مجلس آخر ولم ينو القربة كان إسرافاً فلا يعد به الماء مستعملاً قوله : (فإن كان في مجلس واحد) أي ولم يؤد بالأول عبادة شرع التطهير لها، وإلا فلا يكره قوله : (كره) أي ولو نوى القربة ويكون إسرافاً، والإسراف حرام، ولو على شط نهر قاله السيد ومفاده أن الكراهة تحريمية قوله : (غسل اليد للطعام أو منه) أي بقصد السنة، وإلا لا يستعمل قوله : (لا يصير مستعملاً) لعدم إسقاط فرض، أو إقامة قربة وكذا توضأ بنية التعليم لأن التعليم، وإن كان قربة إلا أنه لم يتعين بالفعل بل يصح بالقول أيضاً، والأصح أن غسالة الميت إذا لم يكن عليه نجاسة مستعملة، کوضوء الحائض بقصد إقامة المستحب، فإن الماء يصير به مستعملاً قوله : (كغسل ثوب ودابة مأكولة) أي طاهرين، وقد قالوا إن عرق الحمار طاهر، والكلب إذا انتفض من الماء فأصاب إنساناً لا ينجسه لأنه طاهر العين، ومقتضى هذا إنه إذا غسلهما تكون غسالتهما طاهرة، وهي مطهرة لعدم موجب الاستعمال قوله : (على الصحيح هو ما عليه العامة، وصحح في الهداية، وكثير من الكتب إنه المذهب كما في البحر ووجهه ما ذكره المصنف بقوله، وسقوط الخ واختار الطحاوي، وبعض مشايخ بلخ، إنه لا يستعمل إلا إذا تنزيهاً لاستقذار النفس له قوله : (أي لا يصح إنَّما فسره بذلك لأنه، لو أبقاه
His statement: (and it is that which is used on the body, etc.) Its apparent meaning is that if he washes a limb of his body for other than janabah or impurity, it becomes used. The most correct is that it does not become used due to lack of fulfilling the obligation, as in al-Bahr. His statement: (for removing an event, even if he does not intend by it a pious act, like the ablution of a hadath without intention, by consensus on the correct view). The author did not mention what is used to fulfill an obligation, such as washing some limbs of his ablution, for it becomes used due to fulfillment of the obligation by agreement, even if it does not remove the event from his limb because it is known that removal and establishment are not divisible, and there is no necessary connection between fulfillment of obligation and removal of the event because the import of fulfillment is non-obligation of repetition, and removal of the event depends on completion. His statement: (the pious act) is an action for which one is rewarded, and there is no reward except with intention. His statement: (seeking closeness to become an act of worship). But if he performed ablution in another gathering and did not intend closeness, it is wasteful and the water is not considered used. His statement: (if it is in one gathering) means and he did not perform with the first an act of worship for which purification is prescribed, otherwise it is not disliked. His statement: (disliked) means even if he intended closeness, and it is wasteful, and waste is haram, even on the bank of a river, as said by al-Sayyid, and its purport is that the dislike is tahrimi. His statement: (washing the hand for food or from it) means with the intention of sunnah, otherwise it does not become used. His statement: (it does not become used) due to non-fulfillment of an obligation or establishment of a pious act. Similarly, ablution with the intention of teaching, because teaching, though a pious act, is not specified by action, but is also valid by speech. The most correct is that the wash water of the deceased if there is no impurity on him is used, like the ablution of a menstruating woman with the intention of performing a mustahabb act, for the water becomes used thereby. His statement: (like washing a garment and an edible animal) meaning both are pure. They said that the sweat of a donkey is pure, and if a dog shakes off water and it hits a person, it does not make him impure because it is pure in itself. The implication of this is that if he washes them, their wash water is pure and is purifying due to absence of cause for being used. His statement: (on the correct view) is what the general public holds, and it is confirmed in al-Hidayah and many books that it is the madhhab, as in al-Bahr, and its reasoning is what the author mentioned by his statement 'and its fall, etc.' Al-Tahawi and some scholars of Balkh chose that it does not become used except when it settles. The benefit of the difference appears when it separates and does not settle, then falls on another limb and flows over it without him taking it with his hand. According to the first, washing that limb with that water is not valid; according to the second, it is valid. Know that concerning the description of used water, some have reported a difference on three narrations. The scholars of Iraq said: no difference is established at all; rather it is pure, not purifying according to all our companions. Shaykh al-Islam said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: it is the chosen view with us, and it is what is mentioned in most of Muhammad's books from our companions, and the verifiers among the scholars of Transoxiana chose it. It is said in al-Mujtaba: the narrations are confirmed from all that it is pure, not purifying, except al-Hasan, and his narration is anomalous and not acted upon, as in Majma' al-Anhur. However, drinking it and kneading with it is disliked tanzihan due to the soul's aversion. His statement: (meaning it is not valid) he explained it thus because, if he left it...
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the ruling on musta'mal (used) water. The matn defined it as water used on the body to remove hadath (ritual impurity). The commentary explains that if water is used for a non-obligatory wash (e.g., washing for coolness), it does not become musta'mal according to the most correct view. Water becomes musta'mal only when it fulfills an obligation (like ablution) or is used for a pious act (qurbah). For ablution without intention, it becomes used because it removes hadath. If one washes only some limbs, it becomes used due to fulfilling the obligation, even though hadath is not fully removed. The commentary also discusses the ruling when the same ablution is done in one gathering vs. separate gatherings—doing it twice in one gathering is wasteful and thus makruh tahrimi. Washing hands for food with sunnah intention makes the water used; otherwise not. The correct view is that water becomes used upon separation from the body, not upon settling (as al-Tahawi held). The difference matters if separated water falls on another limb without being taken by hand. The established Hanafi position is that musta'mal water is pure (tahir) but not purifying (ghayr mutahhir), and this is the relied-upon view. Drinking it is disliked tanzihan due to aversion.

Key Terms
مستعمل (musta'mal): Water that has been used for ritual purification (ablution or bathing) and has separated from the body.
طاهر غير مطهر (tahir ghayr mutahhir): Pure in itself but not capable of removing ritual impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Upon settling after separation
D) Upon completion of wudu

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds musta'mal water as tahir ghayr mutahhir; the majority view that it becomes used upon separation is the operative position.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Understanding Used Water (Ma' Musta'mal) in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining musta'mal water and clarifying its legal status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of ma' musta'mal, explain when it becomes used, and apply the ruling that it is tahir ghayr mutahhir.

Core Idea
Water used with the intention of worship becomes musta'mal upon separation; it is pure but cannot purify.

In Hanafi taharah, the category of water matters deeply. Not all pure water is fit for wudu or ghusl. This lesson unpacks the concept of 'used water' (ma' musta'mal) — water that has been employed in an act of worship and, upon separation, loses its purifying power while remaining pure. We will explore the conditions that trigger this status, the proofs from hadith, and an important intramadhhab dispute about the exact moment when water becomes musta'mal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act makes water musta'mal: using water with the intention of qurba (drawing near to Allah) such as wudu or washing hands before food. The ruling on musta'mal water is that it is tahir (pure) but not mutahhir (purifying).
Intention of qurba is essential; washing without that intention does not make water musta'mal.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites a hadith: 'Wudu before food is a blessing and after it removes madness.' This indicates that washing hands before food is a qurba, so water used for it becomes musta'mal.
The hadith is transmitted as ahad, so its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'qurba' in the hadith implies an act of worship. Since washing hands for food is a sunnah with intention, the water used separates and becomes musta'mal. The change in the water's description (obtaining light) is the basis.
Without qurba, water remains mutahhir.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Tahawi and some scholars of Balkh hold that water becomes musta'mal only when it settles in a place, not upon mere separation. The mainstream view (mufta bihi) is that separation alone is sufficient.
The practical difference: if water separates and falls on another limb without being handled, the first view says it can purify, the second (mufta bihi) says it cannot.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The correct view in the madhhab is that water becomes musta'mal upon separation. This is affirmed by Shaykh al-Islam in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir, by the verifiers of Transoxiana, and is mentioned in the general books of Muhammad al-Shaybani.
An anomalous narration from al-Hasan is rejected.

If it is in one place, it is disliked and the second is not used. Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi discusses the definition and rulings of used water (ma' musta'mal) in Hanafi fiqh. The author begins by stating that water becomes musta'mal when one uses it with the intention of drawing near to Allah (qurba), such as performing wudu in a different place from where he originally had wudu. If he uses it in the same place without performing an act of worship, it is disliked (makruh) and the second use does not make it musta'mal. The text then mentions that washing the hands before and after food is a qurba, supported by a hadith. If water is used for cleaning dirt without qurba, it does not become musta'mal, similar to washing a garment or an animal. The key ruling is that water becomes musta'mal immediately upon separating from the body, even if it does not settle anywhere. This is the correct view (sahih). Before separation, the ruling of usage is suspended due to necessity. The passage also addresses an objection: since the limbs of a person with wudu are not impure, how can water become used? The answer is that the water works to obtain light (nur) like the first time, changing its description. The commentary then discusses the requirement of intention: if one performs wudu in another place without intending qurba, it is wasteful (israf) and the water is not considered used. If wudu is performed in one place, it is disliked only if the first wudu was not done for an act of worship. The dislike is at the level of prohibition (tahrimi). Teaching wudu with intention is not considered a usage because teaching is a qurba but not specified by the act. However, the wash-water of a deceased person or a menstruating woman's wudu for recommended acts does become musta'mal. The passage next clarifies that washing a clean garment or animal does not make water musta'mal; the wash-water remains pure and purifying. An intra-Hanafi difference is mentioned: al-Tahawi and some scholars of Balkh hold that water becomes musta'mal only if it settles, whereas the correct view (sahih) is upon separation. The practice outcome of this difference is whether water that falls on another limb without being handled can be used for purification. The passage concludes that the relied-upon position (mu'tamad) is that musta'mal water is tahir (pure) but not mutahhir (purifying), except for a weak anomalous narration. Drinking it is disliked on account of disgust.

Key Terms
ماء مستعمل (ma' musta'mal): Water that has been used for an act of worship (e.g., wudu) and has separated from the body; it is tahir but not mutahhir.
قربة (qurba): An act intended to draw near to Allah, such as wudu performed with that intention.
طاهر غير طهور (tahir ghayr mutahhir): Pure in itself but not capable of purifying others (i.e., cannot be used for wudu or ghusl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only if the intention is to draw near to Allah.
D) Only if the limbs were najis.

Key Takeaway
Musta'mal water is pure but not purifying; it becomes used upon separation from the body when used with the intention of qurba.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Invalidity of Wudu with Plant Water, Fruit Water, and Cooked Water
Ruling that water from plants and fruits and water altered by cooking is not valid for wudu

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that only mutlaq water is valid for wudu and apply the legal reasoning (qiyas with illa of 'ending of parts') to distinguish between waters that remove real impurity vs. legal impurity.

Core Idea
Only absolute water (ma' mutlaq) is valid for wudu; plant saps, fruit juices, and water altered by cooking are not.

This lesson unpacks a critical ruling from the Hanafi school: you cannot perform wudu with water that has lost its natural properties through mixing with plants, fruits, or cooking. The text from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi explains not only the ruling but the sophisticated legal reasoning behind it—a qiyas (analogy) that distinguishes between removing physical impurity (najasah) and legal impurity (hadath). By the end, you will understand why the Lawgiver's specification of mutlaq water is binding and how to evaluate similar edge cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essential ruling
From the passage, extract the core legal rule: wudu is not valid with water from trees, fruits, or water whose nature has been altered by cooking. Note the key phrase 'لا يصح' (it is not valid).
The ruling is categorical: such waters are not mutlaq and thus cannot remove hadath.
2
Understand the proof structure
The text uses a legal analogy (qiyas). The asl (original case) is mutlaq water, which removes both real impurity and legal impurity. The far' (new case) is plant/fruit/cooked water. The illa (effective cause) is the 'ending of impurity parts by exit with washings' for real impurity, but this does not apply to legal impurity because hadath is not a physical substance.
Recognize that the qiyas is broken: the illa is absent in legal impurity.
3
Analyze the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Al-Zayla'i held that if the water drips naturally without pressing, it may be permissible because the mixing is incomplete. The passage refutes this, stating that even natural drip does not negate the complete mixing that removes the water from being mutlaq.
The majority (al-Hidayah, al-Tanwir, al-Bahr) reject al-Zayla'i's view.
4
Distinguish between cooking for cleaning vs. other purposes
The commentary clarifies that if water is cooked for the purpose of cleaning (e.g., with soap and it remains thin and flowing), it may still be valid for wudu. However, if cooked for other purposes (e.g., chickpeas), it is not valid even if it retains fluidity, because the mixing is complete and the water is no longer mutlaq.
This is a critical refinement that resolves apparent contradictions.
5
Apply the ruling to practical cases
Use the criteria: is the water called 'water' without qualification? Does it retain its natural properties (fluidity, flowability, thirst-quenching, growth)? If not, or if it is from a plant/fruit, then wudu is invalid.
Always check the source and the degree of change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أي لا يصح إنَّما فسره بذلك لأنه لو أبقاه فلم يكن مطلقاً ولو خرج بنفسه من غير عصر كالقاطر من الكرم (في الأظهر) احترز به عما قيل بأنه يجوز بما يقطر بنفسه لأنه ليس لخروجه بلا عصر تأثير في نفي القيد، وصحة نفي الإسم عنه وإنما صح إلحاق المائعات المزيلة بالماء المطلق لتطهير النجاسة الحقيقية لوجود شرط الالتحاق وهي تناهي أجزاء النجاسة بخروجها مع الغسلات وهو منعدم في الحكمية لعدم نجاسة محسوسة بأعضاء المحدث والحدث أمر شرعي له حكم النجاسة لمنع الصلاة معه وعين الشارع لإزالته آلة مخصوصة فلا يمكن إلحاق غيرها بها (ولا) يجوز الوضوء بماء زال طبعه) وهو الرقة والسيلان والإرواء والإنبات بالطبخ) بنحو حمص على حقيقته لا يفيد عدم الصحة وإنما يفيد عدم الحل وقد يجامع الصحة والمقصود الأول قوله : (بماء شجر المراد به مطلق النبات ،کالکرم وورق الهندباء قوله : (وثمر) بالمثلثة ما يثمره النبات، فيشمل جميع الفواكه والأزهار كما في القهستاني قوله: (لكمال امتزاجه) فيه رد على الزيلعي حيث علل جواز رفع الحدث به بأنه لم يكمل امتزاجه، ونظر فيه صاحب البحر قوله: (فلم يكن مطلقاً) إذ لا يطلق عليه اسم الماء بدون قيد قوله : (احترز به عما قيل بأنه الخ) قاله صاحب الهداية: ومشى عليه الزيلعي ومعهما صاحب التنوير قوله : (لأنه ليس لخروجه) علة لقوله : ولا يجوز الخ، وقد علله سابقاً بقوله: لكمال امتزاجه وهو في المال يرجع إلى ما هنا قوله : ( وصحة نفي الإسم أي اسم الماء المطلق حيث لا يقال له ماء بدون قيد، وهو لازم لما قبله لأنه إذا كان لا ينتفي قيده لا يصح إطلاق اسم الماء عليه قوله : (وإنما صح الخ) جواب سؤال حاصله أن الإمام رضي الله تعالى عنه ألحق المائعات بالمطلق في إزالة النجاسة الحقيقية، فمقتضاه أن يلحق المقيد بالمطلق في إزالة الحكمية، إذ لا فرق، وحاصل الجواب بالمنع، وإثبات الفرق قوله : ( لتطهير النجاسة) متعلق بالحاق والأولى التعبير بنفي قوله : الوجود شرط الإلحاق متعلق بصح، وهو علنه قوله : (وهي تناهي الأولى تذكير الضمير كما في نسخ قوله : (بخروجها الباء للسببية وهو متعلق بتناهي قوله: (وهو منعدم في الحكمية) أي شرط الإلحاق الذي التناهي قوله : لعدم نجاسة محسوسة) أي حتى يحكم عليها بالتتامي قوله والحدث أمر شرعي) يصلح جواباً ثانياً :(قوله: (له حكم النجاسة) أي الحقيقية بل هو أعظم لأنه لا يعفى عن قليله قوله : (آلة مخصوصة) وهي إما الماء المطلق، أو خلفه، وهو التراب قوله : ( ولا يجوز الوضوء الخ) الغسل مثل الوضوء في جميع أحكام المياه فلذا لم يصرح به قوله (وهو) الرقة والسيلان اقتصر عليهما في الشرح، وهو الظاهر لأن الأخيرين لا يكونان في ماء البحر الملح، ثم هذا من المصنف ليس على ما ينبغي فإنه متى طبخ بما لا يقصد به النظافة، لا يرفع الحدث، وإن بقي رقيقاً سائلاً لكمال الامتزاج بخلاف ما يقصد به النظافة، فإنه لا يمتنع به رفعه إلا إذا خرج عن رقته وسيلانه، فالفرق بينهما ثابت، وتسوية المصنف بينهما ممنوعة أفاده السيد وغيره قوله : (بالطبخ) قيد به لأنه لو تغير وصف
His statement: (i.e., is not valid) He only interpreted it like that because if he left it, it would not be absolute. And if it comes out by itself without pressing, like the drip from the grapevine (according to the most apparent), he guarded by it against what was said that it is permissible with what drips by itself, because its exit without pressing has no effect in negating the condition, and the correctness of negating the name from it. And the attachment of liquids that remove dirt to absolute water for purifying real impurity is only valid due to the existence of the condition of attachment, which is the ending of the parts of impurity by their exit with the washings. And it is absent in legal impurity because there is no perceptible impurity on the limbs of the person with hadath, and hadath is a legal matter that has the ruling of impurity in preventing prayer, and the Lawgiver specified a particular tool for its removal, so it is not possible to attach others to it. (And) it is not permissible to perform wudu with water whose nature has gone) which is fluidity, flowability, quench-thirst, and growth by cooking) like chickpeas according to its reality; it does not indicate invalidity but rather indicates non-permissibility, and it may coincide with validity, and the first objective is his statement: (with tree water) meaning absolute plant, like grapevine and dandelion leaf. His statement: (and fruit) with a triple consonant letter meaning what the plant yields, thus including all fruits and flowers as in al-Qahastani. His statement: (due to complete mixing) in it is a refutation of al-Zayla'i who explained the permissibility of lifting hadath with it by saying it is not completely mixed, and the author of al-Bahr considered it. His statement: (then it would not be absolute) because the name water is not applied to it without qualification. His statement: (he guarded by it against what was said... etc.) said by the author of al-Hidayah, and al-Zayla'i followed him, and with them is the author of al-Tanwir. His statement: (because its exit) is a cause for his statement: and it is not permissible etc., and he had previously explained it by his statement: due to complete mixing, and it in the property returns to what is here. His statement: (and the correctness of negating the name) meaning the name of absolute water, for it is not called water without qualification, and it is necessary for what preceded because if its condition is not negated, the name water cannot be properly applied. His statement: (and only attachment is valid etc.) is an answer to a question whose summary is that the Imam (may Allah be pleased with him) attached liquids to absolute water in removing real impurity, so its implication would be to attach restricted water to absolute water in removing legal impurity, as there is no difference. The summary of the answer is denial and establishing the difference. His statement: (for purifying impurity) is attached to attachment, and it is better to express with negation. His statement: the existence of the condition of attachment is attached to 'valid', and it is his statement: (which is the ending) the first is masculine pronoun as in copies. His statement: (by their exit) the ba is for cause and it is attached to 'ending'. His statement: (and it is absent in legal impurity) i.e., the condition of attachment which is the ending. His statement: (due to absence of perceptible impurity) i.e., so that it can be judged to have ended. His statement: (and hadath is a legal matter) is suitable as a second answer. His statement: (it has the ruling of impurity) i.e., real impurity, rather it is greater because its small amount is not exempted. His statement: (a particular tool) which is either absolute water or its substitute, which is earth. His statement: (and wudu is not permissible etc.) ghusl is like wudu in all rulings of waters, so he did not mention it. His statement: (and it is) fluidity and flowability; he confined to these two in the commentary, and that is apparent because the last two are not in seawater. Then this from the author is not as it should be, for when it is cooked with what is not intended for cleanliness, it does not remove hadath, even if it remains thin and flowing due to complete mixing, unlike what is intended for cleanliness, for it does not prevent its removal unless it loses its thinness and flowability. So the difference between them is established, and the author's equating them is impermissible, as stated by al-Sayyid and others. His statement: (by cooking) is a restriction, because if its description changes...
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi explains the ruling that wudu is not valid with water obtained from trees (like grapevine sap) or fruits, and with water whose natural properties (fluidity, flowability, thirst-quenching, growth) are removed by cooking (like chickpea water). The author begins by commenting on a previous phrase: if the water is left as is, it would not be absolute water (mutlaq), even if it drips by itself without pressing. The 'most apparent' view is that this does not help make it permissible for wudu because the condition of being absolute water is negated. The text then discusses a key principle: liquids that remove impurities (like soap) can be attached to absolute water for removing real (physical) impurities because they share the condition that the impurity particles end by exiting with the washings. However, for legal impurity (hadath), this analogy does not hold because hadath is not a physical substance; it is a legal ruling that prevents prayer. The Lawgiver appointed a specific tool for its removal: absolute water or its substitute (tayammum with earth). Therefore, other waters (like tree sap or cooked water) cannot be substituted. The commentary then clarifies that tree water refers to any plant liquid, including grapevine and dandelion. Fruit water includes all fruits and flowers. The reason given for prohibition is 'complete mixing' (kamal al-imitizaj), which refutes al-Zayla'i's view that it is permissible because mixing is not complete. The author also engages in a discussion about the difference between water cooked for cleaning purposes (e.g., soap-making) and water cooked for other purposes (e.g., chickpea water). The correct view is that if water is cooked with something not intended for cleanliness, even if it remains thin and flowing, it cannot remove hadath. If cooked for cleanliness, it can remove hadath as long as it retains its thinness and flowability. The author criticizes the matn (Maraqi al-Falah) for equating the two cases, arguing that the difference is established. The passage ends with a restriction that the ruling applies to cooking (bi al-tabkh), implying that if the change in property occurs by other means, the ruling may differ.

Key Terms
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): Pure water that retains its natural properties; it is the only water valid for wudu.
ماء شجر (ma' shajar): Water obtained from trees or plants (e.g., grapevine sap); not mutlaq.
طبخ (tabkh): Cooking that removes the natural properties of water (fluidity, flowability, etc.), making it non-mutlaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, why is water from fruits or trees not valid for wudu?
A) Because it is najis (impure).
B) Because it is not mutlaq water due to complete mixing with plant material.
C) Because it has a bad smell.
D) Because it requires special utensils.

Key Takeaway
For wudu, only mutlaq water (unchanged in its natural properties) is valid; waters from plants, fruits, and water altered by cooking (unless for cleaning retaining fluidity) are not.

Water Cooked with Non-Cleansing Substances: Hanafi Ruling
Explains when water becomes restricted by cooking with non-cleansing solids.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water cooked with non-cleansing solids and distinguish between conditions that render water muqayyad versus those that preserve its mutlaq status.

Core Idea
Water cooked with solids loses its absolute nature and cannot be used for wudu unless it remains thin and flowing.

The Hanafi school holds that water must retain its original properties (raqqah, saylaniyyah) to be considered mutlaq (absolute) and valid for wudu. Cooking with certain substances can remove these properties, rendering the water muqayyad (restricted). In this passage, al-Tahtawi refines the earlier explanation by distinguishing between cleansing and non-cleansing substances, and also addresses an attempted analogy between liquid cleansers and restricted water for ritual impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being discussed (wudu with cooked water) and the hukm (not permissible). Note the key condition: water must remain mutlaq.
The ruling applies to water cooked with solids not intended for cleansing, e.g., chickpeas, lentils.
2
Distinguish between cleansing and non-cleansing substances
Compare cooking with sidr (a cleansing substance) vs. non-cleansing solids. For cleansing substances, water is permissible if it stays thin; for non-cleansing, it becomes muqayyad even if thin.
The purpose of cooking matters: if the substance is intended to clean (like sidr), the water may retain its mutlaq nature if it remains thin.
3
Examine the exception of water dripping by itself
Some claimed that water that drips without squeezing is permissible. Al-Tahtawi refutes this: the extraction method does not affect the water's status; the cooking itself removes the absolute nature.
The condition for mutlaq is about the water's properties, not how it is obtained.
4
Analyze the qiyas rebuttal
Understand the analogy between liquid cleansers (for real impurity) and restricted water (for ritual impurity). The Hanafi response: the analogy fails because ritual impurity has no physical particles; the Lawgiver specified specific tools (water/earth).
This rebuttal strengthens the ruling that restricted water cannot be used for hadath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلم يكن مطلقاً ولو خرج بنفسه من غير عصر كالقاطر من الكرم (في الأظهر) احترز به عما قيل بأنه يجوز بما يقطر بنفسه لأنه ليس لخروجه بلا عصر تأثير في نفي القيد، وصحة نفي الإسم عنه وإنما صح إلحاق المائعات المزيلة بالماء المطلق لتطهير النجاسة الحقيقية لوجود شرط الالحاق وهي تناهي أجزاء النجاسة بخروجها مع الغسلات وهو منعدم في الحكمية لعدم نجاسة محسوسة بأعضاء المحدث والحدث أمر شرعي له حكم النجاسة لمنع الصلاة معه وعين الشارع لإزالته آلة مخصوصة فلا يمكن إلحاق غيرها بها (ولا) يجوز الوضوء بماء زال طبعه) وهو الرقة والسيلان والإرواء والإنبات بالطبخ) بنحو حمص وعدس لأنه إذا برد ثخن كما إذا طبخ بما يقصد به النظافة كالسدر وصار به ثخيناً وإن بقي على الرقة جاز به الوضوء
It is not absolute, even if it comes out by itself without squeezing, like the dripping from a vine (in the most apparent view). He guarded against what was said that it is permissible by what drips by itself, because its coming out without squeezing has no effect in negating the condition, and the correctness of negating the name from it. And rather the validity of annexing the liquid cleansers to absolute water for purifying real impurity is due to the existence of the condition of annexation, which is the ending of the parts of impurity by their exit with the washings, and it is absent in the ruling impurity because there is no sensory impurity on the limbs of the one with hadath, and hadath is a legal matter that has the ruling of impurity in preventing prayer with it, and the Lawgiver specified a particular tool for its removal, so it is not possible to annex others to it. And it is not permissible to perform wudu with water whose nature has been removed (i.e., thinness, fluidity, quenching, and growth by cooking) with something like chickpeas and lentils, because when it cools it thickens, just as if it is cooked with something intended for cleansing like sidr and becomes thick by it, and if it remains thin, wudu with it is permissible.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage from the Hashiya discusses the ruling on water that has been cooked with solid substances not intended for cleansing. The text clarifies that water becomes muqayyad (restricted) when its essential properties of thinness and flow are removed by cooking. The author refines the earlier explanation of the Sharh, distinguishing between cooking with non-cleansing substances (e.g., chickpeas) and cleansing substances (e.g., sidr). If cooked with the former, the water is invalid for wudu even if it remains thin, because the cooking causes complete mixing that alters the water's essence. If cooked with a cleansing substance like sidr, it remains permissible as long as the water retains its thinness and flow, because the purpose is cleaning. The discussion also addresses an objection regarding the analogy between liquids and water for removing ritual impurity versus real impurity. The author supports the Hanafi position that the analogy fails because ritual impurity has no physical trace and requires a specific tool (water or earth).

Key Terms
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): Absolute water that is pure and purifying, without any restriction.
ماء مقيد (ma' muqayyad): Water that has lost its absolute nature due to complete mixing with another substance or removal of its essential properties.
طبخ (tabkh): Cooking process that changes water's essential properties (raqqah, saylaniyyah) by complete mixing.
حقيقة (haqiqa): Real impurity (najasa haqiqiyya) – physical substance that must be removed by washing.
حكمية (hukmiyya): Legal impurity (hadath) – a state that bars prayer but has no physical trace.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on performing wudu with water that has been cooked with chickpeas until it becomes thick upon cooling?
A) It is permissible because the water remains pure.
B) It is not permissible because the water has lost its absolute nature.
C) It is permissible only if no other water is available.
D) It is permissible if chickpeas are pure.

Key Takeaway
Water cooked with non-cleansing solids becomes muqayyad and invalid for wudu; the only exception is if the water remains thin and is cooked with a cleansing substance.

Water Mixed with Pure Solids Without Cooking: Permission for Wudu
Refines the ghalabah criterion with hadith proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water mixed with pure solids and apply the criterion of ghalabah to determine validity for wudu.

Core Idea
Water mixed with pure solids stays pure for wudu if it remains thin and flowing.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi refines the second criterion for water becoming restricted: overwhelming (ghalabah) by a pure solid. You will learn that so long as the water does not lose its thinness and flow, wudu is permissible even if its color, taste, or odor change. The proof comes from prophetic practice with sidr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is using water mixed with a pure solid (like saffron, fruit, or leaves) without cooking for wudu. The ruling is permissibility (ja'iz) as long as the water retains raqqah (thinness) and saylaniyyah (flow).
This is a ruling on the permissibility of wudu, not on the water itself being purifying.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith from Bukhari and Muslim: the Prophet ordered washing a muhrim with water and sidr, and ordered Qays ibn Asim to perform ghusl with water and sidr. This is the primary proof.
The dalil is a hadith ahad, so it is zanni al-thubut.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command to use water mixed with sidr indicates that mixing with a pure solid does not remove the purifying ability of water. The key is that the water retains its essential flow and thinness, as sidr is a cleansing agent.
The hadith shows that even if properties change, the mixture is valid if the water name and effectiveness remain.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is an intra-Hanafi difference: some earlier scholars (like Zaylai) had stricter conditions. The Hashiya refines by emphasizing the retention of thinness and flow as the decisive criterion, not simply change of properties.
Other schools may have different views on when property changes invalidate water.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that wudu is permissible as long as the water remains thin and flowing and the solid is pure. This is supported by the hadith and the principle that the name 'water' remains.
Al-Tahtawi's refinement becomes the operative view for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المخالط بغير طبخ ذكر ملخص ما جعله المحققون ضابطاً في ذلك فقال : ( والغلبة) تحصل (في مخالطة) الماء لشيء من (الجامدات الطاهرات (بإخراج الماء عن رقته فلا ينعصر عن الثوب (و) إخراجه عن (سيلانه) فلا يسيل وسدر) قد يقال غير نحو السدر لا يقاس عليه لأن المقصود به التنظيف فاغتفر فيه تغير الأوصاف، ولا كذلك غيره، ويقال في الحديث الذي بعده كذلك الدر
And since water becomes restricted by one of two things: complete mixing by absorption of plant or cooking as we mentioned, so he explained the second, which is the overwhelming of the mixture by his phrase 'or by the overwhelming of other than it' meaning other than water over it meaning over water. And since overwhelming differs according to the difference of the admixture without cooking, he summarized what the investigators made as a criterion in that, saying: 'And overwhelming occurs in the mixture of water with something from the pure solids by removing the water from its thinness so it cannot be squeezed from the cloth, and removing it from its flow so it does not flow over the limbs like the flow of water. But if it remains on its thinness and flow, then change of all its properties does not harm,' i.e., does not prevent the permissibility of wudu with it, when mixed with a solid without cooking, like saffron, fruit, and leaves of trees. Because of what is in Bukhari and Muslim that the Prophet, peace be upon him, commanded washing the one who was in ihram with water and sidr, and commanded Qays ibn Asim when he embraced Islam to perform ghusl with water and sidr. ... And if it remains thin, wudu with it is permissible, even if it changes its three properties, because it is intended for exaggeration in the desired purpose which is cleanliness, and the name of water remains and its meaning increases which is purification, and thus the sunna in washing the deceased with water boiled with sidr and lye is practiced.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This section from the Hashiya establishes the second criterion for water becoming restricted: overwhelming (ghalabah) by a solid substance mixed without cooking. The main ruling: water mixed with pure solids like saffron, fruit, or leaves remains valid for wudu as long as it does not lose its thinness and flow. Even if its color, taste, or odor change completely, it is still permissible because the name 'water' remains and the change serves the purpose of cleansing. The proof is the hadith from Bukhari and Muslim where the Prophet commanded washing a muhrim with water and sidr, and Qays ibn Asim upon embracing Islam. This indicates that mixing water with pure solids is permitted if the water retains its essential properties. The author refines the earlier position by clarifying the conditions of overwhelming and noting that the hadith's application is limited to cleansing substances; other pure solids may not be analogized. The commentary also addresses objections about complete mixing and the distinction between cooking and simply mixing.

Key Terms
غلبة (ghalabah): Overwhelming of a solid substance mixed with water such that the water loses its thinness and flow.
جامدات طاهر (jamidat tahir): Pure solids that do not themselves cause impurity, e.g., saffron, fruit, leaves.
سدر (sidr): Lote tree leaves, used as a cleansing agent in ghusl and washing the deceased.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hashiya, which condition must water mixed with pure solids without cooking meet to remain valid for wudu?
A) The water must not change color.
B) The water must remain thin and flow easily.
C) The solid must be a cleansing agent like sidr.
D) The water must be boiled first.

Key Takeaway
Water mixed with pure solids remains valid for wudu as long as it retains its thinness and flow, regardless of changes in color, taste, or odor.

Rules of Ghalabah in Mixing Liquids
Explains the principle of ghalabah for mixed liquids and its proof from the Prophet's practice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of ghalabah to determine when mixed water remains valid for wudu based on the appearance of sensory attributes.

Core Idea
If an attribute of a mixed liquid appears dominantly, the water loses its purifying quality for wudu.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's commentary on Maraqi al-Falah sets out the Hanafi rule for when water mixed with another liquid remains usable for wudu. It uses a hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) as evidence and then systematically categorises liquids by the number of attributes, explaining exactly when those attributes cause the water's ruling to change.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling principle
Water mixed with another liquid is valid for wudu unless the added liquid's attribute(s) dominate. Ghalabah is determined by the appearance of sensory qualities (color, taste, smell).
This principle applies only when the original water is pure.
2
Identify the hadith proof
The Prophet (ﷺ) performed ghusl with water containing traces of dough and mallow, showing that minor changes do not affect purity. This establishes that only dominant change matters.
The hadith is ahad but accepted in fiqh.
3
Apply classification by attributes
For liquids with two attributes (e.g., milk: color and taste), if either appears, wudu is invalid. For one attribute (e.g., watermelon: taste only), its appearance invalidates. For three attributes (e.g., vinegar: color, taste, smell), two appearances invalidate; one does not.
If no attribute appears, wudu is valid regardless of quantity.
4
Handle liquids without attributes
For liquids like used water or scentless rose water, which have no distinct attribute, ghalabah is determined by weight. If the non-attribute liquid outweighs pure water, wudu is invalid; if pure water outweighs, it is valid.
Used water is pure but not purifying; this passage treats it as attribute-less.
5
Determine ruling for equal quantities
The mashayikh ruled that when quantities are equal, the mixture follows the ruling of the smaller quantity out of caution, meaning wudu is invalid.
This is not found in zahir al-riwayah but is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واغتسل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بماء فيه أثر العجين وكان صلى الله عليه وسلم يغتسل ويغسل رأسه بالخطمي وهو جنب ويجتزىء بذلك والغلبة تحصل في مخالطة المائعات بظهور وصف واحد كلون فقط أو طعم من مائع له وصفان فقط أي لا ثالث له ومثل ذلك بقوله كاللبن له اللون والطعم فإن لم يوجد أجاز به الوضوء وإن وجد أحدهما لم يجز كما لو كان المخالط له وصف واحد فظهر وصفه كبعض البطيخ ليس له إلا وصف واحد وقوله لا رائحة له زيادة إيضاح لعلمه من بيان الوصفين والغلبة توجد بظهور وصفين من مائع له أوصاف ثلاثة وذلك كالخل له لون وطعم وريح فأي وصفين منها ظهرا منعا صحة الوضوء والواحد منها لا يضر لقلته والغلبة في مخالطة المائع الذي لا وصف له يخالف الماء بلون أو طعم أو ريح كالماء المستعمل فإنه بالاستعمال لم يتغير له طعم ولا لون ولا ريح وهو طاهر في الصحيح ومثله ماء الورد المنقطع الرائحة تكون الغلبة بالوزن لعدم التمييز بالوصف لفقده فإن اختلط رطلان من الماء المستعمل أو ماء الورد الذي انقطعت رائحته برطل من الماء المطلق لا يجوز به الوضوء لغلبة المفيد وبعكسه وهو لو كان الأكثر المطلق جاز به الوضوء وإن استويا لم يذكر حكمه في ظاهر الرواية وقال المشايخ حكمه حكم المغلوب احتياطاً
The Prophet (peace be upon him) performed ghusl with water that had traces of dough, and he (peace be upon him) used to perform ghusl and wash his head with mallow (khatmi) while in a state of janabah, and he considered that sufficient. And overwhelmingness occurs in the mixing of liquids by the appearance of one attribute, such as color only or taste, from a liquid that has only two attributes, i.e., no third. And he gave an example by saying: 'Like milk which has color and taste.' If neither appears, wudu is permissible with it; if one of them appears, it is not permissible. Likewise, if the mixing liquid has only one attribute and that attribute appears, like some watermelon which has only one attribute. And his statement 'it has no smell' is an additional clarification, known from the mention of two attributes. And overwhelmingness occurs by the appearance of two attributes from a liquid that has three attributes, such as vinegar, which has color, taste, and smell. Whichever two of them appear, they prevent the validity of wudu, and one alone does not harm due to its smallness. And overwhelmingness in mixing a liquid that has no attribute that differs from water in color, taste, or smell, like used water (musta'mal), which by use has not changed in taste, color, or smell, and it is pure in the correct view. And likewise, rose water whose smell has disappeared, overwhelmingness is by weight due to inability to distinguish by attribute. If two ratls of used water or rose water without smell are mixed with one ratl of absolute water, wudu is not permissible due to the overwhelmingness of the beneficial (i.e., the non-absolute water). And the opposite, i.e., if the absolute water is more, wudu is permissible. If they are equal, its ruling is not mentioned in the zahir al-riwayah, and the mashayikh said: its ruling is like that of the smaller quantity out of caution.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage, from the hashiya of Al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah, explains the principle of ghalabah (overwhelming quality) when water mixes with other liquids. It begins with a hadith showing that the Prophet (ﷺ) used water with traces of dough and mallow for ghusl, indicating that minor changes do not affect water's purity. The ruling is that if a liquid with two characteristics (like milk with color and taste) mixes with water, wudu is permissible only if neither characteristic appears; if one appears, it indicates the liquid's dominance and wudu becomes impermissible. For a liquid with one characteristic (like watermelon), its appearance alone prevents wudu. For a liquid with three characteristics (like vinegar), if two appear, wudu is impermissible; one is negligible. For liquids that have no distinct characteristic (like used water or scentless rose water), the determination is by weight: if the impure or used water outweighs the pure, wudu is impermissible; if pure outweighs, permissible. If equal, the mashayikh ruled it follows the smaller quantity out of caution. The passage relies on the hadith as proof and extends analogically.

Key Terms
غلبة (ghalabah): Overwhelming dominance of an added liquid's characteristic(s) in a water mixture, determining change in water's ruling.
ماء مستعمل (māʼ mustaʻmal): Used water that has been employed in wudu or ghusl; it has no distinguishing color, taste, or smell but is considered pure in the correct view.
وصف (waṣf): A sensory attribute of a liquid—color, taste, or smell—that can appear when mixed with water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A liquid with two attributes (color and taste) mixes with water. Which condition permits wudu with the mixture?
A) When neither attribute appears.
B) When only color appears.
C) When only taste appears.
D) When both attributes appear.

Key Takeaway
The validity of mixed water for wudu depends on the appearance of the mixed liquid's attributes: if an attribute appears, it is invalid; otherwise, it is valid. For liquids without attributes, weight determines dominance.

Definition of Najis Water and the Ruling on Small Water
Defines impure water and the rule for small water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define najis water, identify the conditions that render water impure, and apply the rule that small standing water becomes impure even without visible trace.

Core Idea
Small standing water becomes impure upon contact with impurity, even without any visible trace.

This lesson introduces the fourth type of water: najis water. The passage defines when water becomes impure, focusing on the concept of 'small' (qalil) water. Understanding this distinction is essential for determining the validity of wudu and ghusl when impurity falls into water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of najis water
Find the passage where it states 'the fourth type of water is impure water' and note the conditions: impurity falls, known with certainty or overwhelming presumption.
The definition excludes small droppings because they are pardoned.
2
Determine the conditions for water to become najis
The water must be standing (rakid) and small (qalil). Qalil is defined as less than 10x10 cubits. If these conditions are met, the water becomes najis even if no trace of impurity appears.
Large water does not become najis unless a trace appears.
3
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a basin size and whether impurity fell, decide if the water is pure or impure. Use the criterion of size and trace.
Remember that for small water, trace is irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و القسم الرابع من المياه ماء نجس وهو الذي حلت أي وقعت فيه نجاسة وعلم وقوعها يقينا أو بغلبة الظن وهذا في غير قليل الأرواث لأنه معفو عنه كما سنذكره وكان الماء راكدا أي ليس جاريا وكان قليلا والقليل هو ما مساحة محله دون عشر في عشر بذراع العامة والذراع يذكر ويؤنث وإن كان قليلا وأصابته نجاسة فينجس وإن لم يظهر أثرها أي النجاسة فيه وأما إذا كان عشرا في عشر بحوض مربع أو ستة وثلاثين في مدور وعمقه أن يكون بحال لا تنكشف أرضه بالغرف منه على الصحيح وقيل يقدر عمقه بذراع أو إلا بظهور وصف للنجاسة فيه حتى موضع الوقوع وبه أخذ مشايخ بلخ قوله هو المفتي به وهو قول عامة المشايخ خانية وهو قول الأكثر نأخذ نوازل وعليه الفتوى كما في شرح الطحاوي وحقق في البحر أن هذا التقدير لا يرجع إلى أصل يعتمد عليه وأن ظاهر الرواية عن الإمام بل عن الثلاثة كما قاله الإمام الرازي التفويض إلى رأي المستعمل فإن غلب على ظنه أنه كثير لا تؤثر فيه النجاسة فهو كثير وإلا وبه
And the fourth type of water is impure water, which is that in which impurity has fallen, and its occurrence is known with certainty or by overwhelming presumption, and this applies other than small droppings because they are pardoned as we will mention. And the water is standing, i.e., not flowing, and it is small. And the small is that whose area is less than ten by ten cubits of the common cubit, and the cubit is used in masculine and feminine. And if it is small and impurity befalls it, it becomes impure even if its trace does not appear. And as for if it is ten by ten in a square basin, or thirty-six in a round one, and its depth is such that its bottom is not exposed by scooping from it according to the correct view, and it is said its depth is estimated by a cubit, or except by the appearance of a characteristic of impurity, even at the spot of occurrence, and the mashayikh of Balkh adopted this. His statement: 'He is the one who gives fatwa' and it is the saying of the general mashayikh of Khaniyah, and it is the saying of the majority, we take from Nawazil, and upon it is the fatwa as in Sharh al-Tahawi. And he verified in al-Bahr that this estimation does not go back to a reliable origin, and that the apparent narration from the Imam, rather from the three as said by Imam al-Razi: it is referred to the opinion of the user. If he strongly thinks that it is large such that impurity does not affect it, then it is large, otherwise... and with it.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage from the hashiya classifies the fourth type of water: najis (impure) water. It states that if impurity falls into standing, small water, it becomes najis even if no trace appears, unless the impurity is from small droppings (which are pardoned). The criterion for 'small' is an area less than 10x10 common cubits (with discussion on cubit type). For larger water (10x10 or more in square, or 36 in round), it does not become najis unless a trace of impurity appears. Depth must be such that the bottom is not exposed when scooping with hands. There is ikhtilaf: the mashayikh of Balkh held that if impurity appears at the spot of occurrence, the whole water becomes najis. The hashiya then notes that this view is the mufta bihi (relied upon for fatwa) according to many later scholars, though al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states that the estimation lacks a sound basis and the original narrations from the Imams refer to the user's judgment based on overwhelming probability.

Key Terms
ماء نجس (māʼ najis): Impure water; water that has become najis due to contact with an impurity, losing its ability to purify.
قليل (qalīl): Small quantity of water: less than 10x10 cubits in area. Such water becomes impure upon contact with impurity even without any trace.
كثير (kathīr): Large quantity of water: area of 10x10 cubits or more. It does not become impure unless a trace of impurity appears.
ذراع (dhirāʻ): Cubit; a unit of length used to measure the basin. The common cubit is used, with a feminine or masculine conjugation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for water to become najis when impurity falls?
A) The water is standing (rakid)
B) The water is small (qalil)
C) A trace of impurity appears in the water
D) The occurrence of impurity is known with certainty or overwhelming presumption

Key Takeaway
Small standing water (less than 10x10 cubits) becomes impure upon contact with impurity, even if no trace is visible.

The Measurement of Large Water and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Defines large water measurement and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the minimum measurement for large water (kathir), explain the depth condition, and analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the spot of impurity occurrence affects the purity of large water.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school defines large water as 10x10 cubits in area, but later scholars dispute whether impurity at the spot alone renders the whole water impure.

This lesson focuses on the definition and rulings for large water (kathir). The passage provides measurements for square and round basins, depth requirements, and then introduces a significant intra-Hanafi disagreement: whether large water becomes impure if impurity is observed at the spot of occurrence. The lesson will trace the original position, the Balkhi view, and the later fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the measurements for large water
Large water is defined as 10x10 cubits for a square basin, and 36 cubits for a round basin. Depth must be such that the bottom is not exposed when scooping with hands.
There is also an opinion that depth should be one cubit.
2
Understand the original Hanafi position
According to the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) from the three Imams, large water does not become impure unless a trace of impurity appears in the water. The decision is left to the user's judgment based on overwhelming probability.
This is the view of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad.
3
Analyze the Balkhi view and its acceptance
The mashayikh of Balkh held that if impurity appears even at the spot of occurrence, the entire water becomes impure. This view is adopted by later scholars and is the mufta bihi.
The hashiya notes that al-Bahr al-Ra'iq criticized the fixed measurement as lacking a reliable origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

A) Large water never becomes impure
B) Large water becomes impure only if trace appears throughout
C) Large water becomes impure if impurity appears at the spot of occurrence
D) Large water becomes impure if the user thinks it is large

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi for large water is that it becomes impure if impurity is seen at the spot of occurrence, even without trace spreading.

Ruling on Water Mixed with Used or Rose Water
Ruling on determining predominant component when pure water mixes with used or rose water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wudu with water mixed with used or rose water and apply the criterion of weight to determine validity.

Core Idea
When pure water mixes with used or rose water, the ruling depends on which is the majority by weight.

When pure water mixes with another type of pure water, such as used water (water previously used for wudu or ghusl) or rose water that has lost its scent, the validity of using that mixture for wudu depends on which component is predominant. Since the mixture lacks any distinctive color or smell, the Hanafi school uses weight as the criterion for predominance. This lesson clarifies the three scenarios – majority of used/rose water, majority of absolute water, and equality – and the practical ruling for each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of water
The passage discusses two categories: absolute pure water (mā' mutlaq) and used water (mā' musta'mal) or rose water that has lost its scent. Understand that used water is considered pure but not purifying (tāhir ghayr tahūr) in the Hanafi school.
Rose water here is treated like used water because its scent is gone.
2
Determine the criterion for predominance
Because the mixture has no color or smell, the criterion for predominance is weight (al-ghalaba bil-wazn). This differs from cases where sensory qualities are present, which are judged by change in those qualities.
Weight is used because the description (color/smell) is absent.
3
Apply the majority rule
If the used/rose water is the majority (e.g., 2 ratls used vs 1 ratl absolute), wudu is not permissible. If the absolute water is the majority, wudu is permissible.
The ruling is based on which component overcomes by weight.
4
Handle the case of equality
The revealed transmission (Zāhir al-Riwāya) does not mention equality. The later mashāyikh (scholars) ruled it as the overcome (invalid) as a precautionary measure (ihtiyāṭ).
Practical caution when the source texts are silent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا لون، ولا ريح وهو طاهر في الصحيح (و) مثله ( ماء الورد المنقطع الرائحة تكون الغلبة (بالوزن) لعدم التمييز بالوصف لفقده (فإن اختلط رطلان) مثلاً (من الماء المستعمل) أو ماء الورد الذي انقطعت رائحته (برطل) من الماء المطلق لا يجوز به الوضوء) لغلبة المفيد (وبعكسه) وهو لو كان الأكثر المطلق (جاز) به الوضوء، وإن استويا لم يذكر حكمه في ظاهر الرواية، وقال المشايخ : حكمه حكم المغلوب احتياطاً
And [if it has] no color and no smell, it is pure according to the correct view. And similarly, rose water whose scent has ceased: the predominance is [determined] by weight due to the inability to distinguish by description because it is absent. So if two ratls, for example, of used water or rose water whose scent has ceased mix with one ratl of absolute water, wudu with it is not permissible because of the predominance of the useful [water]. And the opposite: if the majority is absolute water, wudu with it is permissible. If they are equal, its ruling is not mentioned in the apparent transmission; the mashayikh said: its ruling is the ruling of the overcome, as a precaution.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn regarding water that has changed due to a pure substance, such as rose water or used water. The matn first establishes that water which has changed in color or smell due to a pure substance but has no remaining color or smell is considered pure. Then it discusses the case of mixing: when used water or scentless rose water mixes with pure water, the criterion for validity of wudu is the physical measure (weight) because the sensory qualities (smell, color) are absent. If the used/rose water is the majority, wudu is invalid; if the pure water is the majority, wudu is valid. The condition of equality is not addressed in the revealed transmission (Zahir al-Riwayah), so the later scholars (mashayikh) ruled that it follows the ruling of the overcome majority as a precaution. This is a rule based on practical caution when the legal texts are silent.

Key Terms
ماء مستعمل (mā' musta'mal): Water that has been used in a purification act (wudu or ghusl) and is no longer considered pure for ritual use until it is purified itself.
ماء مطلق (mā' mutlaq): Absolute pure water that is naturally pure and purifying (طهور); not mixed with any impurity.
الغلبة (al-ghalaba): Predominance in quantity (by weight) when two types of water mix.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two ratls of used water mix with one ratl of absolute water, is wudu with this mixture valid according to the Hanafi madhhab?
A) Yes, because the used water is pure.
B) No, because the used water is the majority.
C) Yes, because the absolute water is pure.
D) No, because the mixture is impure.

Key Takeaway
The validity of wudu with mixed water depends on whether the absolute water is the majority by weight; when equal, precaution dictates invalidity.

Definition and Conditions of Impure Water
Defines impure water and sets conditions for it being impure based on quantity and stillness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which water becomes impure in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish between small and large water based on quantity and visible effect.

Core Idea
Small still water is impure upon contact with impurity; large water requires a visible change to become impure.

In this lesson, we examine the fourth category of water: impure water (mā' najis). The passage establishes clear rules for when water becomes impure based on its quantity and stillness. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying purification rulings (taharah) in daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category being defined
The passage defines impure water as water into which a known impurity falls with certainty or strong probability. Note the exception for small amounts of animal dung, which are excused.
2
List conditions for small still water
For small water (less than 10x10 cubits in area) and still (not flowing), the mere falling of impurity renders it impure even if no trace appears.
Small water is defined as that whose area is less than ten by ten cubits using the common surveying cubit.
3
Compare with large water
Large water (ten by ten cubits in a square basin or equivalent) is not impure unless a quality of the impurity (taste, color, smell) appears. Depth must be sufficient so that the bottom is not exposed by scooping.
There is a difference of opinion on exact depth measure; the correct view is that the bottom must not become visible.
4
Understand the practical implication
Small still water becomes impure immediately upon contact with impurity, even without change. Large water only becomes impure if the impurity visibly alters it. This distinction governs use of water for purification.
5
Note the adopted opinion
The mashayikh of Balkh adopted the opinion that for large water, impurity must appear even at the place of fall. This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الرابع من المياه ماء نجس وهو الذي حلت أي وقعت فيه نجاسة وعلم وقوعها يقينا أو بغلبة الظن وهذا في غير قليل الأرواث لأنه معفو عنه كما سنذكره وكان الماء راكداً أي ليس جارياً وكان قليلاً والقليل هو ما مساحة محله دون عشر في عشر بذراع العامة والذراع يذكر ويؤنث وإن كان قليلاً وأصابته نجاسة فينجس وإن لم يظهر أثرها فيه وأما إذا كان عشراً في عشر بحوض مربع أو ستة وثلاثين في مدور وعمقه أن يكون بحال لا تنكشف أرضه بالغرف منه على الصحيح وقيل يقدر عمقه بذراع أو إلا بظهور وصف للنجاسة فيه حتى موضع الوقوع وبه أخذ مشايخ بلخ
And the fourth category of waters is impure water: it is that in which an impurity falls, and its falling is known with certainty or by preponderance of opinion. This is regarding other than small amounts of animal dung, for that is excused as will be mentioned. The water is still (i.e., not flowing) and is small. The small [measure] is that whose area is less than ten by ten cubits of the common cubit, and the cubit is masculine and feminine. If it is small and an impurity strikes it, it becomes impure even if its effect does not appear in it. But if it is ten by ten in a square basin, or thirty-six [square cubits] in a round one, and its depth is such that its bottom is not exposed by scooping with the hands according to the correct view, or it is said its depth is estimated at a cubit, or [it is not impure] except by the appearance of a quality of the impurity in it, even at the place of its fall. And this is what the mashayikh of Balkh adopted.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth category of water: impure (najis) water. It sets three conditions for water to become impure upon contact with impurity: (1) the impurity is not a small amount of animal dung (excused), (2) the water is still (not flowing), and (3) the water is less than the quantity of ten by ten cubits (by the common cubit). For such small still water, the mere fall of impurity, even if no trace appears, renders it impure. For larger water (ten by ten in a square basin or equivalent in a round basin), impurity only affects it if a quality (taste, color, smell) of the impurity appears. The depth must be such that the bottom is not exposed by scooping; if the water is deeper, it is considered large. The mashayikh of Balkh adopted the opinion that impurity must appear even at the place of the fall. The commentary also notes that the cubit used here is the surveying cubit, which is larger than the current cubit, so ten by ten in today's terms is about eight by eight. This passage is critical for understanding Hanafi purity rulings on water.

Key Terms
ماء نجس (mā' najis): Water that has become impure due to contact with impurity; cannot be used for purification.
عشر في عشر (ashr fī 'ashr): The minimum dimension of a square basin for water to be considered large; ten cubits by ten cubits using the surveying cubit.
ذراع العامة (dhirā' al-'āmma): The common cubit used for measurement; in this context, the surveying cubit (ذراع المساحة) which is larger than the cubit of cloth (ذراع الكرباس).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a piece of najasah falls into a square basin that is exactly ten by ten cubits (surveying cubit) but its depth is such that the bottom is not exposed when scooping, and no change is observed in the water, is the water considered impure according to the Hanafi madhhab?
A) Yes, because the impurity fell into it.
B) No, because it is large water and no effect appeared.
C) Yes, because the water is still.
D) No, because the basin is square.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the purity of water depends on its quantity, stillness, and the appearance of impurity trace.

Categories of Su'r and Doubted Water
Begins discussion on doubted water and defines su'r

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the fifth type of water (doubted in purifying ability) and define su'r (leftover water) in Hanafi fiqh, and explain the condition regarding the animal's mother.

Core Idea
Water drunk by a donkey from a she-ass is pure but doubted in purifying ability; su'r is the leftover of such water from a small source.

In Hanafi fiqh, water is categorized into types based on purity and purifying ability. This passage introduces the fifth type: water that is certainly pure but whose purifying ability is doubtful, specifically when a donkey or mule from a she-ass drinks from it. It also defines su'r, the leftover water after an animal drinks from a small, non-flowing water source. Understanding these categories is crucial for correctly applying rulings on purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fifth water type
Locate the phrase 'والنوع الخامس ماء مشكوك في طهوريته'. Recognize that this water is pure (طاهر) but its purifying ability is doubted (مشكوك في طهوريته).
This is a unique Hanafi category; other schools may not have this distinction.
2
Understand the condition for doubt
The doubt applies specifically to water drunk by a donkey or mule whose mother is a domesticated she-ass (أتان), not a mare (رمكة). The mother's species determines the ruling.
The principle 'العبرة للأم' (consideration is for the mother) is key.
3
Define su'r (leftover water)
The passage defines su'r as the leftover water after an animal drinks from a small water source (less than 10x10 cubits) that is not flowing. The term is specific to such water.
Su'r is borrowed metaphorically for other remnants, but its technical meaning is restricted.
4
Recognize the legal implication
The ruling on su'r follows the category of the animal: if the animal is pure, the su'r is pure; if impure, it is impure; for doubted animals (like the mule from a she-ass), the su'r is doubted in purifying ability.
This affects whether the water can be used for purification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنوع الخامس ماء مشكوك في طهوريته لا في طهارته وهو ما شرب منه حمار أو بغل وكانت أمه أتانا لا رمكة لأن العبرة للام كما سنذكره في الأسآر إن شاء الله تعالى فصل في بيان أحكام السؤر والماء القليل الذي بينا قدره بدون عشر في عشر ولم يكن جارياً إذا شرب منه حيوان يكون على أحد أربعة أقسام و ما أبقاه بعد شربه يسمى سؤراً بهمز عينه ويستعار الاسم لبقية
And the fifth type is water doubted in its purifying ability, not in its purity, and it is what a donkey or mule has drunk from, and its mother was an [domesticated] she-ass, not a mare, because the consideration is for the mother, as we will mention in the section on su'r, God willing. A chapter on explaining the rulings of su'r. And the small water that we have explained its measure as less than ten by ten and not flowing, if an animal drinks from it, it is of one of four categories. And what it leaves after drinking is called su'r, with the letter 'ayn bearing a hamza, and the name is borrowed for the remainder.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage introduces the fifth type of water: water doubted in its purifying ability (طهورية) but not in its purity (طهارة). It is water from which a donkey or mule has drunk, provided the animal's mother was a domesticated she-ass (atan) and not a mare (ramaka), because the legal consideration follows the mother. This water is pure in itself but its ability to purify other things is doubtful, so one should use it with caution. Then the text begins a new chapter on the rulings of su'r (leftover water after an animal drinks). It defines su'r as the small amount of water left after an animal drinks from it. The name is also used metaphorically for other remnants. The discussion of the four categories of animals is to follow, but the passage here only gives the definition.

Key Terms
ماء مشكوك (mā' mashkūk): Water whose purifying ability (طهورية) is doubtful, though its purity (طهارة) is certain; e.g., water drunk by a donkey or mule from a domesticated she-ass.
سؤر (su'r): The leftover water after an animal drinks from it, specifically from small water (<10x10 cubits) and not flowing.
أتان (atān): A domesticated female donkey (she-ass), as opposed to a mare (ramaka).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal definition of su'r?
A) The remaining water after any animal drinks from any water source
B) The leftover water after an animal drinks from small, non-flowing water
C) The water that is doubted in purifying ability
D) The first gulp of water an animal takes

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes water based on purity and purifying ability, and the leftovers (su'r) are categorized according to the animal's legal status.
