A) They must be washed separately
B) They are purified automatically when the well is purified
C) They remain impure until sun-dried
D) They are replaced

Key Takeaway
A mouse in a well requires 20 wajib buckets; the water and tools are purified together, reflecting the principle that secondary impurities are removed with their source.

Ruling on Animal Droppings Not Defiling a Well
States that a well is not defiled by droppings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on animal droppings in a well and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
The droppings of halal animals are pure; a well remains unpolluted by them.

A common question about water purity is whether a well becomes impure when animal droppings fall into it. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi stance: the well remains pure, and no purification is needed. We will examine the specific terms used for different droppings and the underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the act is the dropping of animal waste into a well. The Hanafi ruling is that this does not defile the well.
2
Understand the linguistic terms
Note the specific Arabic terms: ba'r (camels/sheep), rawth (horses/mules/donkeys), khithy (cows). These distinctions are important for legal precision.
3
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school considers all animal droppings impure. Hanafi distinguishes based on the permissibility of eating the animal's meat.
4
Apply the ruling
In practice, if any of these droppings fall into a well, the water remains pure and can be used without any special treatment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تنجس البئر بالبعر وهو للإبل، والغنم وبعر يبعر من حد منع والروث للفرس والبغل والحمار من حد نصر والخثي بكسر الخاء واحد الأخشاء للبقر من باب ضرب ولا فرق بين آبار الأمصار والفلوات في ذلك
And the well is not defiled by 'bahr' – which is for camels and sheep, and bahr is derived from the verb 'yab'uru' (from the paradigm of man'a). And 'rawth' is for horses, mules, and donkeys (from the paradigm of nasara). And 'khithy' (with a kasrah on the kha') – one of 'akhtha' – for cows (from the paradigm of daraba). And there is no difference between wells of cities and deserts in this regard.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement regarding the purity of a well when animal droppings fall into it. The matn clarifies that different terms are used for different animals: 'bahr' for camel and sheep dung, 'rawth' for horse, mule, and donkey dung, and 'khithy' for cow dung. The commentary explains the verbal paradigms for these nouns. The key ruling: these droppings do not defile (tunjis) the well, meaning the well remains pure. This applies equally to wells in urban areas and those in open deserts. The reason is that these droppings are considered pure (tahir) in the Hanafi school because they come from animals whose meat is permissible to eat, or they are from animals whose droppings are not generally considered najis.

Key Terms
البعر (al-ba'r): Droppings of camels and sheep.
الروث (al-rawth): Droppings of horses, mules, and donkeys.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Multiple Animals in a Well
Intra-Hanafi dispute on water amounts for multiple fallen animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi views on water amounts for multiple fallen animals and apply the rule that the larger animal subsumes the smaller.

Core Idea
When multiple animals fall into a well, the larger animal's ruling prevails; the smaller is included in the larger.

This lesson addresses a specific intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding how much water must be drawn when multiple animals or animals of intermediate size fall into a well. The ruling is not simply additive; instead, the scale depends on the number and size of the animals. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi methodology of tarjih (preference) and the authoritative status of zahir al-riwayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is determining the amount of water to remove from a well when multiple animals (mice, cat, dog, chicken, sheep) fall in. The ruling is wajib: the water must be purified by removing a specific number of buckets.
The base ruling for a single mouse is known (e.g., 20 buckets); this lesson builds on that.
2
State Abu Yusuf's view
Abu Yusuf holds that four mice are like one mouse (same amount), five mice are like a chicken, up to nine mice like a chicken, and ten mice like a sheep.
His scale is based on number thresholds.
3
State Muhammad's view
Muhammad holds that three to five mice are like a cat, and six mice are like a dog. This is the zahir al-riwayah (apparent narration) and is preferred.
Muhammad uses size categories rather than strict counts.
4
Apply the subsumption rule
The text provides a general rule: animals between a mouse and a cat follow the mouse ruling; between a cat and a dog follow the cat ruling. If two animals of different categories fall, the larger subsumes the smaller (e.g., mouse and cat together count as cat).
This prevents additive calculations.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Al-Tahtawi confirms that Muhammad's view is the relied-upon position because it is from zahir al-riwayah. Differences in borderline cases (e.g., five mice) exist but the preferred view is clear.
Tarjih is based on authority of the transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وروي عن أبي يوسف أن الأربع من الفيران كفارة واحدة والخمس كالدجاجة إلى التسع والعشر كالشاة وقال محمد الثلاث إلى الخمس كالهرة والست كالكلب وهو ظاهر الرواية، وما كان بين الفأرة والهرة فحكمه حكم الفأرة، وما كان بين الهرة والكلب فحكمه حكم الهرة، وإن وقع فأرة وهرة فهما كهرة ويدخل الأقل في الأكثر
And it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that four mice are equivalent to one kaffarah (expiation) [i.e., one mouse ruling?] and five are like a chicken, up to nine, and ten are like a sheep. And Muhammad said: three to five are like a cat, and six are like a dog – and that is the apparent (zahir) narration. And whatever is between a mouse and a cat, its ruling is the ruling of a mouse; and whatever is between a cat and a dog, its ruling is the ruling of a cat. And if a mouse and a cat fall, both are like a cat, and the smaller is included in the larger.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage records an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the amount of water to draw when multiple animals or animals of intermediate size fall into a well. According to Abu Yusuf: four mice are considered like one mouse (maybe 20 buckets?), five mice are like a chicken (40 buckets), up to nine mice are like a chicken, and ten mice are like a sheep (a larger animal requiring more water). According to Muhammad: three to five mice are like a cat (40 buckets), six are like a dog (a larger amount, possibly 60 or 120? The text does not specify the exact number for a dog, but by analogy it is more than a cat). The 'zahir al-riwayah' is said to be Muhammad's view. Then a general rule is given: animals between the size of a mouse and a cat follow the mouse's ruling; those between a cat and a dog follow the cat's ruling. If a mouse and a cat fall together, the combined ruling is that of the cat (the larger), and the smaller is subsumed. This helps determine the amount for mixed cases.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation – in this context, it refers to the ruling for a single mouse (maybe 20 buckets) as a baseline.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent/well-known transmission in the Hanafi school, from the six core books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the zahir al-riwayah (Muhammad's view), what is the ruling if four mice fall into a well?
A) They are like four mice individually (20 each = 80 buckets)
B) They are like a cat (40 buckets)
C) They are like a chicken (40 buckets)
D) They are like a dog (60 buckets)

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when multiple animals fall into a well, the larger animal's ruling dominates and the smaller is subsumed; the preferred view is that three to five mice are like a cat, and six mice are like a dog.
